	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Francis Bacon</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>

<div class="NavHead" style="font-size: 105%; background: transparent; text-align: left;">Influenced</div>
<ul class="NavContent" style="list-style: none none; margin-left: 0; text-align: left; font-size: 105%; margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0; line-height: inherit;"><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><a href="/wiki/Basil_Montagu" title="Basil Montagu">Basil Montagu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A9distes" title="Encyclopédistes">Encyclopédistes</a>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a></span>, <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/William_Petty" title="William Petty">William Petty</a></span></div></li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2"></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Francis Bacon, 1st Viscount St Alban</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-title_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-title-5">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Privy_Counsellor_(England)" class="mw-redirect" title="Privy Counsellor (England)">PC</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Counsel" title="Queen&#39;s Counsel">QC</a></span> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="/eɪ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;face&#39;">eɪ</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/ən/: &#39;on&#39; in &#39;button&#39;">ən</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> 22 January 1561&#160;&#8211;&#32;9 April 1626) was an English philosopher and statesman who served as <a href="/wiki/Attorney_General_for_England_and_Wales" title="Attorney General for England and Wales">Attorney General</a> and as <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">Lord Chancellor</a> of England. His works are credited with developing the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a> and remained influential through the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific revolution">scientific revolution</a>. 
</p><p>Bacon has been called the father of <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> His works argued for the possibility of scientific knowledge based only upon <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">inductive reasoning</a> and careful observation of events in <a href="/wiki/Nature_(philosophy)" title="Nature (philosophy)">nature</a>. Most importantly, he argued science could be achieved by use of a sceptical and methodical approach whereby scientists aim to avoid misleading themselves. Although his practical ideas about such a method, the <i><a href="/wiki/Baconian_method" title="Baconian method">Baconian method</a></i>, did not have a long-lasting influence, the general idea of the importance and possibility of a sceptical methodology makes Bacon the father of the scientific method. This method was a new rhetorical and theoretical framework for science, the practical details of which are still central in debates about science and <a href="/wiki/Methodology" title="Methodology">methodology</a>.
</p><p>Francis Bacon was a patron of libraries and developed a functional system for the cataloging of books by dividing them into three categories—history, poetry, and philosophy—which could further be divided into more specific subjects and subheadings. Bacon was educated at <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">Trinity College, Cambridge</a>, where he rigorously followed the medieval curriculum, largely in Latin.
</p><p>Bacon was the first recipient of the <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_counsel" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen&#39;s counsel">Queen's counsel</a> designation, which was conferred in 1597 when Elizabeth I of England reserved Bacon as her legal advisor. After the accession of James VI and I in 1603,  Bacon was <a href="/wiki/Knight_Bachelor" title="Knight Bachelor">knighted</a>. He was later created <a href="/wiki/Baron_Verulam" title="Baron Verulam">Baron Verulam</a> in 1618<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson1878200_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson1878200-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> and Viscount St. Alban in 1621.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeltonen2007_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeltonen2007-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Because he had no heirs, both titles became extinct upon his death in 1626, at 65 years.  Bacon died of <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, with one account by <a href="/wiki/John_Aubrey" title="John Aubrey">John Aubrey</a> stating that he had contracted the condition while studying the effects of freezing on the preservation of meat. He is buried at <a href="/wiki/St_Michael%27s_Church,_St_Albans" title="St Michael&#39;s Church, St Albans">St Michael's Church, St Albans</a>, Hertfordshire.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Biography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:18-year_old_Francis_Bacon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/18-year_old_Francis_Bacon.jpg/220px-18-year_old_Francis_Bacon.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/18-year_old_Francis_Bacon.jpg/330px-18-year_old_Francis_Bacon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/18-year_old_Francis_Bacon.jpg/440px-18-year_old_Francis_Bacon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="476" data-file-height="558" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:18-year_old_Francis_Bacon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The young Francis Bacon. Inscription around his head reads: <i>Si tabula daretur digna animum mallem</i>, <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a> for "If one could but paint his mind". <a href="/wiki/National_Portrait_Gallery,_London" title="National Portrait Gallery, London">National Portrait Gallery, London</a></div></div></div>
<p>Francis Bacon was born on 22 January 1561 at <a href="/wiki/York_House,_Strand" title="York House, Strand">York House</a> near the <a href="/wiki/Strand,_London" title="Strand, London">Strand</a> in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, the son of <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Bacon_(Lord_Keeper)" title="Nicholas Bacon (Lord Keeper)">Sir Nicholas Bacon</a> (<a href="/wiki/Lord_Keeper_of_the_Great_Seal" title="Lord Keeper of the Great Seal">Lord Keeper of the Great Seal</a>) by his second wife, <a href="/wiki/Anne_Bacon" title="Anne Bacon">Anne (Cooke) Bacon</a>, the daughter of the noted <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">Renaissance humanist</a> <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Cooke" title="Anthony Cooke">Anthony Cooke</a>. His mother's sister was married to <a href="/wiki/William_Cecil,_1st_Baron_Burghley" title="William Cecil, 1st Baron Burghley">William Cecil, 1st Baron Burghley</a>, making Burghley Bacon's uncle.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Biographers believe that Bacon was educated at home in his early years owing to poor health, which would plague him throughout his life. He received tuition from John Walsall, a graduate of Oxford with a strong leaning toward <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritanism</a>. He went up to <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">Trinity College</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a> on 5 April 1573 at the age of 12,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> living for three years there, together with his older brother <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Bacon_(1558%E2%80%931601)" title="Anthony Bacon (1558–1601)">Anthony Bacon</a> under the personal tutelage of <a href="/wiki/John_Whitgift" title="John Whitgift">Dr John Whitgift</a>, future <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>. Bacon's education was conducted largely in Latin and followed the medieval curriculum. He was also educated at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Poitiers" title="University of Poitiers">University of Poitiers</a>. It was also at <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a> that Bacon first met <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Elizabeth I">Queen Elizabeth</a>, who was impressed by his precocious intellect, and was accustomed to calling him "The young lord keeper".<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His studies brought him to the belief that the methods and results of science as then practised were erroneous. His reverence for <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> conflicted with his rejection of <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelian philosophy</a>, which seemed to him barren, disputatious and wrong in its objectives.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:York_Water_Gate.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/York_Water_Gate.jpg/220px-York_Water_Gate.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/York_Water_Gate.jpg/330px-York_Water_Gate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/York_Water_Gate.jpg/440px-York_Water_Gate.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="541" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:York_Water_Gate.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Italianate" class="mw-redirect" title="Italianate">Italianate</a> York Water Gate – the entry to York House, built about 1626, the year of Bacon's death</div></div></div>
<p>On 27 June 1576, he and Anthony entered <i>de societate magistrorum</i> at <a href="/wiki/Gray%27s_Inn" title="Gray&#39;s Inn">Gray's Inn</a>. A few months later, Francis went abroad with Sir <a href="/wiki/Amias_Paulet" title="Amias Paulet">Amias Paulet</a>, the English ambassador at Paris, while Anthony continued his studies at home. The state of government and society in France under <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_France" title="Henry III of France">Henry III</a> afforded him valuable political instruction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamsonMitchell1911136_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamsonMitchell1911136-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> For the next three years he visited <a href="/wiki/Blois" title="Blois">Blois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poitiers" title="Poitiers">Poitiers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tours" title="Tours">Tours</a>, Italy, and Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-travels_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-travels-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> During his travels, Bacon studied language, statecraft, and civil law while performing routine diplomatic tasks. On at least one occasion he delivered diplomatic letters to England for <a href="/wiki/Francis_Walsingham" title="Francis Walsingham">Walsingham</a>, Burghley, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Dudley,_1st_Earl_of_Leicester" title="Robert Dudley, 1st Earl of Leicester">Leicester</a>, as well as for the queen.<sup id="cite_ref-travels_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-travels-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The sudden death of his father in February 1579 prompted Bacon to return to England. Sir Nicholas had laid up a considerable sum of money to purchase an estate for his youngest son, but he died before doing so, and Francis was left with only a fifth of that money.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamsonMitchell1911136_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamsonMitchell1911136-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Having borrowed money, Bacon got into debt. To support himself, he took up his residence in law at Gray's Inn in 1579,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamsonMitchell1911136_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamsonMitchell1911136-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> his income being supplemented by a grant from his mother Lady Anne of the manor of <a href="/wiki/Marks_(manor_house)" title="Marks (manor house)">Marks</a> near <a href="/wiki/Romford" title="Romford">Romford</a> in Essex, which generated a rent of £46.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parliamentarian">Parliamentarian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Parliamentarian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Francis_Bacon_statue,_Gray%27s_Inn.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Francis_Bacon_statue%2C_Gray%27s_Inn.jpg/220px-Francis_Bacon_statue%2C_Gray%27s_Inn.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Francis_Bacon_statue%2C_Gray%27s_Inn.jpg/330px-Francis_Bacon_statue%2C_Gray%27s_Inn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Francis_Bacon_statue%2C_Gray%27s_Inn.jpg/440px-Francis_Bacon_statue%2C_Gray%27s_Inn.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Francis_Bacon_statue,_Gray%27s_Inn.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Francis Bacon's statue at <a href="/wiki/Gray%27s_Inn" title="Gray&#39;s Inn">Gray's Inn</a>, South Square, London</div></div></div>
<p>Bacon stated that he had three goals: to uncover truth, to serve his country, and to serve his church. He sought to further these ends by seeking a prestigious post. In 1580, through his uncle, <a href="/wiki/William_Cecil,_1st_Baron_Burghley" title="William Cecil, 1st Baron Burghley">Lord Burghley</a>, he applied for a post at court that might enable him to pursue a life of learning, but his application failed. For two years he worked quietly at <a href="/wiki/Gray%27s_Inn" title="Gray&#39;s Inn">Gray's Inn</a>, until he was admitted as an <a href="/wiki/Outer_barrister" class="mw-redirect" title="Outer barrister">outer barrister</a> in 1582.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His parliamentary career began when he was elected <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of Parliament (UK)">MP</a> for <a href="/wiki/Bossiney_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Bossiney (UK Parliament constituency)">Bossiney, Cornwall</a>, in a <a href="/wiki/By-election" title="By-election">by-election</a> in 1581. In 1584 he took his seat in <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">parliament</a> for <a href="/wiki/Melcombe_Regis_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" class="mw-redirect" title="Melcombe Regis (UK Parliament constituency)">Melcombe</a> in Dorset, and in 1586 for <a href="/wiki/Taunton_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Taunton (UK Parliament constituency)">Taunton</a>. At this time, he began to write on the condition of parties in the church, as well as on the topic of philosophical reform in the lost tract <i>Temporis Partus Maximus</i>. Yet he failed to gain a position that he thought would lead him to success.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamsonMitchell1911136_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamsonMitchell1911136-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> He showed signs of sympathy to Puritanism, attending the sermons of the Puritan chaplain of Gray's Inn and accompanying his mother to the <a href="/wiki/Temple_Church" title="Temple Church">Temple Church</a> to hear <a href="/wiki/Walter_Travers" title="Walter Travers">Walter Travers</a>. This led to the publication of his earliest surviving tract, which criticised the English church's suppression of the Puritan clergy. In the Parliament of 1586, he openly urged execution for the Catholic <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary, Queen of Scots</a>.
</p><p>About this time, he again approached his powerful uncle for help; this move was followed by his rapid progress at the bar. He became a <a href="/wiki/Bencher" title="Bencher">bencher</a> in 1586 and was elected a <a href="/wiki/Reader_(Inns_of_Court)" title="Reader (Inns of Court)">Reader</a> in 1587, delivering his first set of lectures in Lent the following year. In 1589, he received the valuable appointment of <a href="/wiki/Reversion_(law)" title="Reversion (law)">reversion</a> to the Clerkship of the <a href="/wiki/Star_Chamber" title="Star Chamber">Star Chamber</a>, although he did not formally take office until 1608; the post was worth £1,600 a year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamsonMitchell1911136_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamsonMitchell1911136-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeltonen2007_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeltonen2007-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1588 he became MP for <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Liverpool (UK Parliament constituency)">Liverpool</a> and then for <a href="/wiki/Middlesex_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Middlesex (UK Parliament constituency)">Middlesex</a> in 1593. He later sat three times for <a href="/wiki/Ipswich_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Ipswich (UK Parliament constituency)">Ipswich</a> (1597, 1601, 1604) and once for <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Cambridge University (UK Parliament constituency)">Cambridge University</a> (1614).<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He became known as a liberal-minded reformer, eager to amend and simplify the law. Though a friend of the crown, he opposed feudal privileges and dictatorial powers. He spoke against religious persecution. He struck at the House of Lords in its usurpation of the Money Bills. He advocated for the union of England and Scotland, which made him a significant influence toward the consolidation of the United Kingdom; and he later would advocate for the integration of Ireland into the Union. Closer constitutional ties, he believed, would bring greater peace and strength to these countries.<sup id="cite_ref-letters_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-letters-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ecc.015_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ecc.015-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Final_years_of_the_Queen.27s_reign"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years_of_the_Queen's_reign">Final years of the Queen's reign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Final years of the Queen&#039;s reign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bacon soon became acquainted with <a href="/wiki/Robert_Devereux,_2nd_Earl_of_Essex" title="Robert Devereux, 2nd Earl of Essex">Robert Devereux, 2nd Earl of Essex</a>, Queen Elizabeth's favourite.<sup id="cite_ref-Hammer_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hammer-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> By 1591 he acted as the earl's confidential adviser.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamsonMitchell1911136_13-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamsonMitchell1911136-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hammer_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hammer-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1592 he was commissioned to write a tract in response to the Jesuit <a href="/wiki/Robert_Parsons_(Jesuit)" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Parsons (Jesuit)">Robert Parson</a>'s anti-government polemic, which he titled <i>Certain observations made upon a libel</i>, identifying England with the ideals of democratic <a href="/wiki/History_of_Athens" title="History of Athens">Athens</a> against the belligerence of Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacon took his third parliamentary seat for <a href="/wiki/Middlesex" title="Middlesex">Middlesex</a> when in February 1593 Elizabeth summoned Parliament to investigate a Roman Catholic plot against her. Bacon's opposition to a bill that would levy triple subsidies in half the usual time offended the Queen: opponents accused him of seeking popularity, and for a time the Court excluded him from favour.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:StatueOfFrancisBacon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/StatueOfFrancisBacon.jpg/220px-StatueOfFrancisBacon.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/StatueOfFrancisBacon.jpg/330px-StatueOfFrancisBacon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/StatueOfFrancisBacon.jpg/440px-StatueOfFrancisBacon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="768" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:StatueOfFrancisBacon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Memorial to Francis Bacon, in the chapel of <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">Trinity College, Cambridge</a></div></div></div>
<p>When the office of <a href="/wiki/Attorney-General_for_England_and_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Attorney-General for England and Wales">Attorney General</a> fell vacant in 1594, Lord Essex's influence was not enough to secure the position for Bacon and it was given to Sir <a href="/wiki/Edward_Coke" title="Edward Coke">Edward Coke</a>. Likewise, Bacon failed to secure the lesser office of <a href="/wiki/Solicitor-General_for_England_and_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Solicitor-General for England and Wales">Solicitor General</a> in 1595, the Queen pointedly snubbing him by appointing Sir <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Fleming_(judge)" title="Thomas Fleming (judge)">Thomas Fleming</a> instead.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeltonen2007_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeltonen2007-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> To console him for these disappointments, Essex presented him with a property at <a href="/wiki/Twickenham" title="Twickenham">Twickenham</a>, which Bacon subsequently sold for £1,800.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1597 Bacon became the first <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Counsel" title="Queen&#39;s Counsel">Queen's Counsel</a> designate, when Queen Elizabeth reserved him as her legal counsel.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In 1597, he was also given a patent, giving him precedence at the Bar.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>  Despite his designations, he was unable to gain the status and notoriety of others. In a plan to revive his position he unsuccessfully courted the wealthy young widow Lady <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Hatton" title="Elizabeth Hatton">Elizabeth Hatton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> His courtship failed after she broke off their relationship upon accepting marriage to Sir Edward Coke, a further spark of enmity between the men.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In 1598 Bacon was arrested for debt. Afterward, however, his standing in the Queen's eyes improved. Gradually, Bacon earned the standing of one of the learned counsels.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamsonMitchell1911137_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamsonMitchell1911137-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> His relationship with the Queen further improved when he severed ties with Essex—a shrewd move, as Essex would be executed for treason in 1601.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With others, Bacon was appointed to investigate the charges against Essex. A number of Essex's followers confessed that Essex had planned a rebellion against the Queen.<sup id="cite_ref-Character_Assassination_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Character_Assassination-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>  Bacon was subsequently a part of the legal team headed by the <a href="/wiki/Attorney_General_for_England_and_Wales" title="Attorney General for England and Wales">Attorney General</a> Sir Edward Coke at Essex's treason trial.<sup id="cite_ref-Character_Assassination_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Character_Assassination-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> After the execution, the Queen ordered Bacon to write the official government account of the trial, which was later published as <i>A DECLARATION of the Practices and Treasons attempted and committed by Robert late Earle of Essex and his Complices, against her Majestie and her Kingdoms ...</i> after Bacon's first draft was heavily edited by the Queen and her ministers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamsonMitchell1911138_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamsonMitchell1911138-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to his personal secretary and chaplain, <a href="/wiki/William_Rawley" title="William Rawley">William Rawley</a>, as a judge Bacon was always tender-hearted, "looking upon the examples with the eye of severity, but upon the person with the eye of pity and compassion". And also that "he was free from malice", "no revenger of injuries", and "no defamer of any man".<sup id="cite_ref-Rawley_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rawley-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="James_I_comes_to_the_throne">James I comes to the throne</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: James I comes to the throne">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The succession of <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="James I of England">James I</a> brought Bacon into greater favour. He was <a href="/wiki/Knight_Bachelor" title="Knight Bachelor">knighted</a> in 1603. In another shrewd move, Bacon wrote his <i>Apologies</i> in defence of his proceedings in the case of Essex, as Essex had favoured James to succeed to the throne.
</p><p>The following year, during the course of the uneventful first parliament session, Bacon married <a href="/wiki/Alice_Barnham" title="Alice Barnham">Alice Barnham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamsonMitchell1911139_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamsonMitchell1911139-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In June 1607 he was at last rewarded with the office of solicitor general.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeltonen2007_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeltonen2007-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The following year, he began working as the Clerkship of the <a href="/wiki/Star_Chamber" title="Star Chamber">Star Chamber</a>. Despite a generous income, old debts still could not be paid. He sought further promotion and wealth by supporting King James and his arbitrary policies.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Francis_Bacon,_Viscount_St_Alban_from_NPG_(2).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Portrait of Sir Francis Bacon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Francis_Bacon%2C_Viscount_St_Alban_from_NPG_%282%29.jpg/220px-Francis_Bacon%2C_Viscount_St_Alban_from_NPG_%282%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Francis_Bacon%2C_Viscount_St_Alban_from_NPG_%282%29.jpg/330px-Francis_Bacon%2C_Viscount_St_Alban_from_NPG_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Francis_Bacon%2C_Viscount_St_Alban_from_NPG_%282%29.jpg/440px-Francis_Bacon%2C_Viscount_St_Alban_from_NPG_%282%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="2993" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Francis_Bacon,_Viscount_St_Alban_from_NPG_(2).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sir Francis Bacon, c. 1618</div></div></div>
<p>In 1610 the fourth session of James's first <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_England_and_the_English_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="James I of England and the English Parliament">parliament</a> met. Despite Bacon's advice to him, James and the Commons found themselves at odds over royal prerogatives and the king's embarrassing extravagance. The House was finally dissolved in February 1611. Throughout this period Bacon managed to stay in the favour of the king while retaining the confidence of the Commons.
</p><p>In 1613 Bacon was finally appointed <a href="/wiki/Attorney_General_for_England_and_Wales" title="Attorney General for England and Wales">attorney general</a>, after advising the king to shuffle judicial appointments. As attorney general, Bacon, by his zealous efforts—which included torture—to obtain the conviction of Edmund Peacham for treason, raised legal controversies of high constitutional importance;<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> and successfully prosecuted <a href="/wiki/Robert_Carr,_1st_Earl_of_Somerset" title="Robert Carr, 1st Earl of Somerset">Robert Carr, 1st Earl of Somerset</a>, and his wife, <a href="/wiki/Frances_Howard,_Countess_of_Somerset" class="mw-redirect" title="Frances Howard, Countess of Somerset">Frances Howard, Countess of Somerset</a>, for murder in 1616. The so-called Prince's Parliament of April 1614 objected to Bacon's presence in the seat for <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Cambridge (UK Parliament constituency)">Cambridge</a> and to the various royal plans that Bacon had supported. Although he was allowed to stay, parliament passed a law that forbade the attorney general to sit in parliament. His influence over the king had evidently inspired resentment or apprehension in many of his peers. Bacon, however, continued to receive the King's favour, which led to his appointment in March 1617 as temporary Regent of England (for a period of a month), and in 1618 as <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">Lord Chancellor</a>. On 12 July 1618 the king created Bacon <b><a href="/wiki/Baron_Verulam" title="Baron Verulam">Baron Verulam</a></b>, of <a href="/wiki/Verulamium" title="Verulamium">Verulam</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Peerage_of_England" title="Peerage of England">Peerage of England</a>;  he then became known as Francis, Lord Verulam.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeltonen2007_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeltonen2007-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacon continued to use his influence with the king to mediate between the throne and Parliament, and in this capacity he was further elevated in the same peerage, as <b><a href="/wiki/Viscount_St_Alban" class="mw-redirect" title="Viscount St Alban">Viscount St Alban</a></b>, on 27 January 1621.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lord_Chancellor_and_public_disgrace">Lord Chancellor and public disgrace</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Lord Chancellor and public disgrace">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bacon_vs_Parliament.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Bacon_vs_Parliament.jpg/220px-Bacon_vs_Parliament.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Bacon_vs_Parliament.jpg/330px-Bacon_vs_Parliament.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Bacon_vs_Parliament.jpg/440px-Bacon_vs_Parliament.jpg 2x" data-file-width="501" data-file-height="326" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bacon_vs_Parliament.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Francis Bacon and the members of the Parliament on the day of his political fall</div></div></div>
<p>Bacon's public career ended in disgrace in 1621. After he fell into debt, a parliamentary committee on the administration of the law charged him with 23 separate counts of corruption. His lifelong enemy, Sir <a href="/wiki/Edward_Coke" title="Edward Coke">Edward Coke</a>, who had instigated these accusations,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> was one of those appointed to prepare the charges against the chancellor.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> To the lords, who sent a committee to enquire whether a confession was really his, he replied, "My lords, it is my act, my hand, and my heart; I beseech your lordships to be merciful to a broken reed." He was sentenced to a fine of £40,000 and committed to the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a> at the king's pleasure; the imprisonment lasted only a few days and the fine was remitted by the king.<sup id="cite_ref-mpkm_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mpkm-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> More seriously, parliament declared Bacon incapable of holding future office or sitting in parliament. He narrowly escaped undergoing <a href="/wiki/Cashiering" title="Cashiering">degradation</a>, which would have stripped him of his titles of nobility. Subsequently, the disgraced viscount devoted himself to study and writing.
</p><p>There seems little doubt that Bacon had accepted gifts from litigants, but this was an accepted custom of the time and not necessarily evidence of deeply corrupt behaviour.<sup id="cite_ref-pz_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pz-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> While acknowledging that his conduct had been lax, he countered that he had never allowed gifts to influence his judgement and, indeed, he had on occasion given a verdict against those who had paid him. He even had an interview with King James in which he assured:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The law of nature teaches me to speak in my own defence: With respect to this charge of bribery I am as innocent as any man born on St. Innocents Day. I never had a bribe or reward in my eye or thought when pronouncing judgment or order... I am ready to make an oblation of myself to the King</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>17 April 1621<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>He also wrote the following to Buckingham:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>My mind is calm, for my fortune is not my felicity. I know I have clean hands and a clean heart, and I hope a clean house for friends or servants; but Job himself, or whoever was the justest judge, by such hunting for matters against him as hath been used against me, may for a time seem foul, especially in a time when greatness is the mark and accusation is the game.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler1885347_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler1885347-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>The true reason for his acknowledgement of guilt is the subject of debate, but some authors speculate that it may have been prompted by his sickness, or by a view that through his fame and the greatness of his office he would be spared harsh punishment.  He may even have been blackmailed, with a threat to charge him with sodomy, into confession.<sup id="cite_ref-pz_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pz-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The British jurist <a href="/wiki/Basil_Montagu" title="Basil Montagu">Basil Montagu</a> wrote in Bacon's defence, concerning the episode of his public disgrace:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Bacon has been accused of servility, of dissimulation, of various base motives, and their filthy brood of base actions, all unworthy of his high birth, and incompatible with his great wisdom, and the estimation in which he was held by the noblest spirits of the age. It is true that there were men in his own time, and will be men in all times, who are better pleased to count spots in the sun than to rejoice in its glorious brightness. Such men have openly libelled him, like Dewes and Weldon, whose falsehoods were detected as soon as uttered, or have fastened upon certain ceremonious compliments and dedications, the fashion of his day, as a sample of his servility, passing over his noble letters to the Queen, his lofty contempt for the Lord Keeper Puckering, his open dealing with Sir Robert Cecil, and with others, who, powerful when he was nothing, might have blighted his opening fortunes for ever, forgetting his advocacy of the rights of the people in the face of the court, and the true and honest counsels, always given by him, in times of great difficulty, both to Elizabeth and her successor. When was a "base sycophant" loved and honoured by piety such as that of Herbert, Tennison, and Rawley, by noble spirits like Hobbes, Ben Jonson, and Selden, or followed to the grave, and beyond it, with devoted affection such as that of Sir Thomas Meautys.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_beliefs">Religious beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Religious beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Bacon was a devout <a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a>. He believed that philosophy and the natural world must be studied inductively, but argued that we can only study arguments for the existence of God. Information on his attributes (such as nature, action, and purposes) can only come from special revelation. But Bacon also held that knowledge was cumulative, that study encompassed more than a simple preservation of the past. "Knowledge is the rich storehouse for the glory of the Creator and the relief of man's estate," he wrote. In his Essays, he affirms that "a little philosophy inclineth man's mind to atheism, but depth in philosophy bringeth men's minds about to religion."<sup id="cite_ref-EssayesOrCovnsels_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EssayesOrCovnsels-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacon's idea of <a href="/wiki/Novum_Organum#The_Idols_(Idola)" title="Novum Organum">idols of the mind</a> may have self-consciously represented an attempt to Christianise science at the same time as developing a new, reliable scientific method; Bacon gave worship of <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> as an example of the <a href="/wiki/Idola_tribus" title="Idola tribus">idola tribus</a> fallacy, hinting at the religious dimensions of his critique of the idols.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_to_Alice_Barnham">Marriage to Alice Barnham</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Marriage to Alice Barnham">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>When he was 36, Bacon courted <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Hatton" title="Elizabeth Hatton">Elizabeth Hatton</a>, a young widow of 20. Reportedly, she broke off their relationship upon accepting marriage to a wealthier man, Bacon's rival, Sir <a href="/wiki/Edward_Coke" title="Edward Coke">Edward Coke</a>. Years later, Bacon still wrote of his regret that the marriage to Hatton had not taken place.<sup id="cite_ref-Dodd-Life-Story_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dodd-Life-Story-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the age of 45, Bacon married <a href="/wiki/Alice_Barnham" title="Alice Barnham">Alice Barnham</a>, the 14-year-old daughter of a well-connected London <a href="/wiki/Alderman" title="Alderman">alderman</a> and MP. Bacon wrote two sonnets proclaiming his love for Alice. The first was written during his courtship and the second on his wedding day, 10 May 1606. When Bacon was appointed lord chancellor, "by special Warrant of the King", Lady Bacon was given precedence over all other Court ladies. Bacon's personal secretary and chaplain, William Rawley, wrote in his biography of Bacon that his marriage was one of "much conjugal love and respect", mentioning a robe of honour that he gave to Alice and which "she wore unto her dying day, being twenty years and more after his death".<sup id="cite_ref-Rawley_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rawley-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alice_Barnham.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Alice_Barnham.gif/220px-Alice_Barnham.gif" decoding="async" width="220" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9c/Alice_Barnham.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="288" data-file-height="354" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alice_Barnham.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Engraving of <a href="/wiki/Alice_Barnham" title="Alice Barnham">Alice Barnham</a></div></div></div>
<p>However, an increasing number of reports circulated about friction in the marriage, with speculation that this may have been due to Alice's making do with less money than she had once been accustomed to. It was said that she was strongly interested in fame and fortune, and when household finances dwindled, she complained bitterly. Bunten wrote in her <i>Life of Alice Barnham</i><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> that, upon their descent into debt, she went on trips to ask for financial favours and assistance from their circle of friends. Bacon disinherited her upon discovering her secret romantic relationship with <a href="/wiki/Sir_John_Underhill" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir John Underhill">Sir John Underhill</a>. He subsequently rewrote his will, which had previously been very generous—leaving her lands, goods, and income—and instead revoked it all.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexuality">Sexuality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Sexuality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Several authors believe that despite his marriage Bacon was primarily attracted to the same sex.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Forker,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> for example, has explored the "historically documentable sexual preferences" of both Francis Bacon and King James I and concluded they were both orientated to "masculine love", a contemporary term that "seems to have been used exclusively to refer to the sexual preference of men for members of their own gender."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>The well-connected antiquary <a href="/wiki/John_Aubrey" title="John Aubrey">John Aubrey</a> noted in his <i><a href="/wiki/Brief_Lives" title="Brief Lives">Brief Lives</a></i> concerning Bacon, "He was a <a href="/wiki/Pederast" class="mw-redirect" title="Pederast">Pederast</a>. His <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(mythology)" title="Ganymede (mythology)">Ganimeds</a> and Favourites tooke Bribes".<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> ("Pederast" in <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> diction meant generally "homosexual" rather than specifically a lover of minors; "ganimed" derives from the mythical prince abducted by Zeus to be his cup-bearer and bed warmer.)&#160;
</p><p>The Jacobean antiquarian, Sir <a href="/wiki/Simonds_D%27Ewes" class="mw-redirect" title="Simonds D&#39;Ewes">Simonds D'Ewes</a> (Bacon's fellow Member of Parliament) implied there had been a question of bringing him to trial for buggery,<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> which his brother Anthony Bacon had also been charged with.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In his Autobiography and Correspondence, D'Ewes discusses Bacon's love for his Welsh serving-men, in particular a "very effeminate-faced youth" whom he calls "his catamite and bedfellow" ("catamite" is a corruption of "Ganymede"). The diary entry for 3 May 1621—the date of Bacon's censure by Parliament—reveals the full extent of Bacon's homosexuality.
</p><p>This conclusion has been disputed by others, who point to lack of consistent evidence, and consider the sources to be more open to interpretation.<sup id="cite_ref-Character_Assassination_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Character_Assassination-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>  Publicly, at least, Bacon distanced himself from the idea of homosexuality. In his <i><a href="/wiki/New_Atlantis" title="New Atlantis">New Atlantis</a></i>, he described his <a href="/wiki/Utopian" class="mw-redirect" title="Utopian">utopian</a> island as being "the chastest nation under heaven", and "as for <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality" title="Homosexuality">masculine love</a>, they have no touch of it".<sup id="cite_ref-Atlantis1627_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Atlantis1627-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:20040912-001-francis-bacon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/20040912-001-francis-bacon.jpg/220px-20040912-001-francis-bacon.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/20040912-001-francis-bacon.jpg/330px-20040912-001-francis-bacon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/20040912-001-francis-bacon.jpg/440px-20040912-001-francis-bacon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1704" data-file-height="2272" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:20040912-001-francis-bacon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Monument to Bacon at his burial place, <a href="/wiki/St._Michael%27s_Church,_St._Albans" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Michael&#39;s Church, St. Albans">St Michael's Church</a> in <a href="/wiki/St_Albans" title="St Albans">St Albans</a></div></div></div><p>
On 9 April 1626, Francis Bacon died of <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a> while at Arundel mansion at <a href="/wiki/Highgate" title="Highgate">Highgate</a> outside London.<sup id="cite_ref-death_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-death-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> An influential account of the circumstances of his death was given by John Aubrey's <i>Brief Lives</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-death_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-death-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Aubrey's vivid account, which portrays Bacon as a martyr to experimental scientific method, had him journeying to Highgate through the snow with the King's physician when he is suddenly inspired by the possibility of using the snow to preserve meat:</p><blockquote><p>They were resolved they would try the experiment presently. They alighted out of the coach and went into a poor woman's house at the bottom of Highgate hill, and bought a fowl, and made the woman <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/exenterate" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:exenterate">exenterate</a> it.</p></blockquote><p>
After stuffing the fowl with snow, Bacon contracted a fatal case of <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>. Some people, including Aubrey, consider these two contiguous, possibly coincidental events as related and causative of his death:</p><blockquote><p>The Snow so chilled him that he immediately fell so extremely ill, that he could not return to his Lodging … but went to the Earle of Arundel's house at Highgate, where they put him into … a damp bed that had not been layn-in … which gave him such a cold that in 2 or 3 days as I remember Mr Hobbes told me, he died of Suffocation.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>Aubrey has been criticised for his evident credulousness in this and other works; on the other hand, he knew <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a>, Bacon's fellow-philosopher and friend.
Being unwittingly on his deathbed, the philosopher dictated his last letter to his absent host and friend <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Howard,_21st_Earl_of_Arundel" title="Thomas Howard, 21st Earl of Arundel">Lord Arundel</a>:</p><blockquote><p>My very good Lord,—I was likely to have had the fortune of <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Caius Plinius the elder</a>, who lost his life by trying an experiment about the burning of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Vesuvius" title="Mount Vesuvius">Mount Vesuvius</a>; for I was also desirous to try an experiment or two touching the conservation and induration of bodies. As for the experiment itself, it succeeded excellently well; but in the journey between London and Highgate, I was taken with such a fit of <a href="/wiki/Cough" title="Cough">casting</a> as I know not whether it were the Stone, or some surfeit or cold, or indeed a touch of them all three.  But when I came to your Lordship's House, I was not able to go back, and therefore was forced to take up my lodging here, where your housekeeper is very careful and diligent about me, which I assure myself your Lordship will not only pardon towards him, but think the better of him for it.  For indeed your Lordship's House was happy to me, and I kiss your noble hands for the welcome which I am sure you give me to it. I know how unfit it is for me to write with any other hand than mine own, but by my troth my fingers are so disjointed with sickness that I cannot steadily hold a pen.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><span id="William_Rawley"></span>Another account appears in a biography by William Rawley, Bacon's personal secretary and chaplain:</p><blockquote><p>He died on the ninth day of April in the year 1626, in the early morning of the day then celebrated for our Saviour's resurrection, in the sixty-sixth year of his age, at the Earl of Arundel's house in Highgate, near London, to which place he casually repaired about a week before; God so ordaining that he should die there of a gentle fever, accidentally accompanied with a great cold, whereby the defluxion of <a href="/wiki/Rheum" title="Rheum">rheum</a> fell so plentifully upon his breast, that he died by suffocation.<sup id="cite_ref-Rawley-bio_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rawley-bio-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>He was buried in <a href="/wiki/St._Michael%27s_Church,_St._Albans" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Michael&#39;s Church, St. Albans">St Michael's church</a> in St Albans. At the news of his death, over 30 great minds collected together their eulogies of him, which were then later published in Latin.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> 
He left personal assets of about £7,000 and lands that realised £6,000 when sold.<sup id="cite_ref-BGL_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BGL-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> His debts amounted to more than £23,000, equivalent to more than £3m at current value.<sup id="cite_ref-BGL_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BGL-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy_and_works">Philosophy and works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Philosophy and works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Works_by_Francis_Bacon" title="Works by Francis Bacon">Works by Francis Bacon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bacon_-_Sylva_sylvarum,_1658_-_3887855_301161_00006.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bacon_-_Sylva_sylvarum%2C_1658_-_3887855_301161_00006.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Bacon_-_Sylva_sylvarum%2C_1658_-_3887855_301161_00006.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="333" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bacon_-_Sylva_sylvarum%2C_1658_-_3887855_301161_00006.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Bacon_-_Sylva_sylvarum%2C_1658_-_3887855_301161_00006.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bacon_-_Sylva_sylvarum%2C_1658_-_3887855_301161_00006.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Bacon_-_Sylva_sylvarum%2C_1658_-_3887855_301161_00006.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2834" data-file-height="4289" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bacon_-_Sylva_sylvarum,_1658_-_3887855_301161_00006.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bacon, <i>Sylva sylvarum</i></div></div></div>
<p>Francis Bacon's philosophy is displayed in the vast and varied writings he left, which might be divided into three great branches:
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Works_by_Francis_Bacon#Scientific_works" title="Works by Francis Bacon">Scientific works</a></i> – in which his ideas for a universal reform of knowledge into scientific methodology and the improvement of mankind's state using the Scientific method are presented.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Works_by_Francis_Bacon#Religious_and_literary_works" title="Works by Francis Bacon">Religious and literary works</a></i> – in which he presents his moral philosophy and theological meditations.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Works_by_Francis_Bacon#Juridical_works" title="Works by Francis Bacon">Juridical works</a></i> – in which his reforms in English Law are proposed.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frontispiece_to_%27The_History_of_the_Royal-Society_of_London%27.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Frontispiece_to_%27The_History_of_the_Royal-Society_of_London%27.jpg/220px-Frontispiece_to_%27The_History_of_the_Royal-Society_of_London%27.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Frontispiece_to_%27The_History_of_the_Royal-Society_of_London%27.jpg/330px-Frontispiece_to_%27The_History_of_the_Royal-Society_of_London%27.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e0/Frontispiece_to_%27The_History_of_the_Royal-Society_of_London%27.jpg 2x" data-file-width="401" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frontispiece_to_%27The_History_of_the_Royal-Society_of_London%27.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Frontispiece to 'The History of <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal-Society</a> of London', picturing Bacon (in the right) among the founding influences of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Society</a>. National Portrait Gallery, London</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science">Science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bacon's seminal work <i><a href="/wiki/Novum_Organum" title="Novum Organum">Novum Organum</a></i> was influential in the 1630s and 1650s among scholars, in particular Sir <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Browne" title="Thomas Browne">Thomas Browne</a>, who in his encyclopedia <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudodoxia_Epidemica" title="Pseudodoxia Epidemica">Pseudodoxia Epidemica</a></i> (1646–72) frequently adheres to a Baconian approach to his scientific enquiries. This book entails the basis of the Scientific Method as a means of observation and induction. During the <a href="/wiki/English_Restoration" class="mw-redirect" title="English Restoration">Restoration</a>, Bacon was commonly invoked as a guiding spirit of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> founded under Charles II in 1660.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> During the 18th-century <a href="/wiki/French_Enlightenment" class="mw-redirect" title="French Enlightenment">French Enlightenment</a>, Bacon's non-metaphysical approach to science became more influential than the dualism of his French contemporary <a href="/wiki/Descartes" class="mw-redirect" title="Descartes">Descartes</a>, and was associated with criticism of the <i><a href="/wiki/Ancien_regime" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancien regime">ancien regime</a></i>. In 1733 <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> introduced him to a French audience as the "father" of the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>, an understanding which had become widespread by the 1750s.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In the 19th century his emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Induction_(philosophy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Induction (philosophy)">induction</a> was revived and developed by <a href="/wiki/William_Whewell" title="William Whewell">William Whewell</a>, among others. He has been reputed as the "Father of Experimental Philosophy".<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He also wrote a long treatise on Medicine, <i>History of Life and Death</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> with natural and experimental observations for the prolongation of life.
</p><p>One of his biographers, the historian <a href="/wiki/William_Hepworth_Dixon" title="William Hepworth Dixon">William Hepworth Dixon</a>, states: "Bacon's influence in the modern world is so great that every man who rides in a train, sends a telegram, follows a <a href="/wiki/Steam_plough" class="mw-redirect" title="Steam plough">steam plough</a>, sits in an easy chair, crosses the channel or the Atlantic, eats a good dinner, enjoys a beautiful garden, or undergoes a painless surgical operation, owes him something."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1902 <a href="/wiki/Hugo_von_Hofmannsthal" title="Hugo von Hofmannsthal">Hugo von Hofmannsthal</a> published a fictional letter, known as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lord_Chandos_Letter" title="The Lord Chandos Letter">The Lord Chandos Letter</a></i>, addressed to Bacon and dated 1603, about a writer who is experiencing a crisis of language.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lord_Bacon_-_the_guiding_spirit_of_colonization_scheme.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Lord_Bacon_-_the_guiding_spirit_of_colonization_scheme.jpg/170px-Lord_Bacon_-_the_guiding_spirit_of_colonization_scheme.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Lord_Bacon_-_the_guiding_spirit_of_colonization_scheme.jpg/255px-Lord_Bacon_-_the_guiding_spirit_of_colonization_scheme.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dc/Lord_Bacon_-_the_guiding_spirit_of_colonization_scheme.jpg 2x" data-file-width="262" data-file-height="333" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lord_Bacon_-_the_guiding_spirit_of_colonization_scheme.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Newfoundland stamp, which reads "Lord Bacon – the guiding spirit in colonization scheme"</div></div></div>
<p>Bacon played a leading role in establishing the <a href="/wiki/British_colonies_in_North_America" class="mw-redirect" title="British colonies in North America">British colonies in North America</a>, especially in <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Carolinas" title="The Carolinas">the Carolinas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland</a> in northeastern Canada. His government report on "The Virginia Colony" was submitted in 1609. In 1610 Bacon and his associates received a charter from the king to form <i>the Tresurer and the Companye of Adventurers and planter of the Cittye of London and Bristoll for the Collonye or plantacon in Newfoundland</i>, and sent <a href="/wiki/John_Guy_(governor)" title="John Guy (governor)">John Guy</a> to found a colony there.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, the third President of the United States, wrote: "Bacon, <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">Locke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newton</a>. I consider them as the three greatest men that have ever lived, without any exception, and as having laid the foundation of those superstructures which have been raised in the <a href="/wiki/Physical_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Physical science">Physical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moral_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Moral science">Moral sciences</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1910 Newfoundland issued a postage stamp to commemorate Bacon's role in establishing the colony. The stamp describes Bacon as "the guiding spirit in Colonization Schemes in 1610".<sup id="cite_ref-Dodd-Life-Story_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dodd-Life-Story-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Moreover, some scholars believe he was largely responsible for the drafting, in 1609 and 1612, of two charters of government for the Virginia Colony.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> William Hepworth Dixon considered that Bacon's name could be included in the list of Founders of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although few of his proposals for law reform were adopted during his lifetime, Bacon's legal legacy was considered by the magazine <i><a href="/wiki/New_Scientist" title="New Scientist">New Scientist</a></i> in 1961 as having influenced the drafting of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Code" title="Napoleonic Code">Napoleonic Code</a> as well as the law reforms introduced by 19th-century British Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Sir_Robert_Peel" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Robert Peel">Sir Robert Peel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The historian <a href="/wiki/William_Hepworth_Dixon" title="William Hepworth Dixon">William Hepworth Dixon</a> referred to the Napoleonic Code as "the sole embodiment of Bacon's thought", saying that Bacon's legal work "has had more success abroad than it has found at home", and that in France "it has blossomed and come into fruit".<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Harvey_Wheeler" title="Harvey Wheeler">Harvey Wheeler</a> attributed to Bacon, in <i>Francis Bacon's Verulamium—the Common Law Template of The Modern in English Science and Culture</i>, the creation of these distinguishing features of the modern <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> system:
</p>
<ul><li>using cases as repositories of evidence about the "unwritten law";</li>
<li>determining the relevance of precedents by exclusionary principles of evidence and logic;</li>
<li>treating opposing legal briefs as adversarial hypotheses about the application of the "unwritten law" to a new set of facts.</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Francis_Bacon_in_the_Library_of_Congress.Jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Francis_Bacon_in_the_Library_of_Congress.Jpeg/220px-Francis_Bacon_in_the_Library_of_Congress.Jpeg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Francis_Bacon_in_the_Library_of_Congress.Jpeg/330px-Francis_Bacon_in_the_Library_of_Congress.Jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Francis_Bacon_in_the_Library_of_Congress.Jpeg/440px-Francis_Bacon_in_the_Library_of_Congress.Jpeg 2x" data-file-width="725" data-file-height="725" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Francis_Bacon_in_the_Library_of_Congress.Jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Francis Bacon in the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a>, Washington, DC.</div></div></div>
<p>As late as the 18th century some juries still declared the law rather than the facts, but already before the end of the 17th century <a href="/wiki/Sir_Matthew_Hale" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Matthew Hale">Sir Matthew Hale</a> explained modern common law adjudication procedure and acknowledged Bacon as the inventor of the process of discovering unwritten laws from the evidences of their applications.  The method combined empiricism and inductivism in a new way that was to imprint its signature on many of the distinctive features of modern <a href="/wiki/English_society" title="English society">English society</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>  <a href="/wiki/Paul_H._Kocher" title="Paul H. Kocher">Paul H. Kocher</a> writes that Bacon is considered by some jurists to be the father of modern <a href="/wiki/Jurisprudence" title="Jurisprudence">Jurisprudence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jurisprudence_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jurisprudence-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacon is commemorated with a statue in <a href="/wiki/Gray%27s_Inn" title="Gray&#39;s Inn">Gray's Inn</a>, South Square in London where he received his legal training, and where he was elected Treasurer of the Inn in 1608.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>More recent scholarship on Bacon's jurisprudence has focused on his advocating torture as a legal recourse for the crown.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Bacon himself was not a stranger to the torture chamber: in his various legal capacities in both Elizabeth I's and James I's reigns, Bacon was listed as a commissioner on five torture warrants. In 1613(?), in a letter addressed to King James I on the question of torture's place within English law, Bacon identifies the scope of torture as a means to further the investigation of threats to the state: "In the cases of treasons, torture is used for discovery, and not for evidence."<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> For Bacon, torture was not a punitive measure, an intended form of state repression, but instead offered a modus operandi for the government agent tasked with uncovering acts of treason.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization_of_knowledge">Organization of knowledge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Organization of knowledge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Francis Bacon developed the idea that a classification of knowledge must be universal while handling all possible resources. In his progressive view, humanity would be better if the access to educational resources were provided to the public, hence the need to organise it. His approach to learning reshaped the Western view of knowledge theory from an individual to a social interest.
The original classification proposed by Bacon organised all types of knowledge in three general groups: history, poetry, and philosophy. He did that based on his understanding of how information is processed: memory, imagination, and reason, respectively. His methodical approach to the categorization of knowledge goes hand-in-hand with his principles of scientific methods. Bacon’s writings were the starting point for <a href="/wiki/William_Torrey_Harris" title="William Torrey Harris">William Torrey Harris</a> classification system for libraries in the United States by the second half of the 1800s.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_debates">Historical debates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Historical debates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bacon_and_Shakespeare">Bacon and Shakespeare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Bacon and Shakespeare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Baconian_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Baconian theory">Baconian theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bacon%27s_cipher" title="Bacon&#39;s cipher">Bacon's cipher</a></div>
<p>The Baconian hypothesis of <a href="/wiki/Shakespearean_authorship" class="mw-redirect" title="Shakespearean authorship">Shakespearean authorship</a>, first proposed in the mid-19th century, contends that Francis Bacon wrote some or even all of the plays conventionally attributed to <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occult_theories">Occult theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Occult theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Occult_theories_about_Francis_Bacon" title="Occult theories about Francis Bacon">Occult theories about Francis Bacon</a></div>
<p>Francis Bacon often gathered with the men at Gray's Inn to discuss politics and philosophy, and to try out various theatrical scenes that he admitted writing.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Bacon's alleged connection to the <a href="/wiki/Rosicrucianism" title="Rosicrucianism">Rosicrucians</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">Freemasons</a> has been widely discussed by authors and scholars in many books.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> However, others, including <a href="/wiki/Daphne_du_Maurier" title="Daphne du Maurier">Daphne du Maurier</a> in her biography of Bacon, have argued that there is no substantive evidence to support claims of involvement with the Rosicrucians.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frances_Yates" title="Frances Yates">Frances Yates</a><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> does not make the claim that Bacon was a Rosicrucian, but presents evidence that he was nevertheless involved in some of the more closed intellectual movements of his day. She argues that Bacon's movement for the advancement of learning was closely connected with the German Rosicrucian movement, while Bacon's <i>New Atlantis</i> portrays a land ruled by Rosicrucians. He apparently saw his own movement for the advancement of learning to be in conformity with Rosicrucian ideals.<sup id="cite_ref-Yates_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yates-88">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bacon-Rose.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Bacon-Rose.jpg/220px-Bacon-Rose.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Bacon-Rose.jpg/330px-Bacon-Rose.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Bacon-Rose.jpg/440px-Bacon-Rose.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="322" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bacon-Rose.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An old volume of Francis Bacon and a rose</div></div></div>
