<p>The link between Bacon's work and the Rosicrucians' ideals which Yates allegedly found was the conformity of the purposes expressed by the <a href="/wiki/Rosicrucian_Manifestos" class="mw-redirect" title="Rosicrucian Manifestos">Rosicrucian Manifestos</a> and Bacon's plan of a "<a href="/wiki/Works_by_Francis_Bacon#The_Great_Instauration" title="Works by Francis Bacon">Great Instauration</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-Yates_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yates-88">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> for the two were calling for a reformation of both "divine and human understanding",<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> as well as both had in view the purpose of mankind's return to the "state before the Fall".<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another major link is said to be the resemblance between Bacon's <i>New Atlantis</i> and the German Rosicrucian <a href="/wiki/Johann_Valentin_Andreae" class="mw-redirect" title="Johann Valentin Andreae">Johann Valentin Andreae</a>'s <i>Description of the Republic of Christianopolis</i> (1619).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndreae1619_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndreae1619-93">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>  Andreae describes a utopic island in which Christian <a href="/wiki/Theosophy_(Boehmian)" title="Theosophy (Boehmian)">theosophy</a> and applied science ruled, and in which the spiritual fulfilment and intellectual activity constituted the primary goals of each individual, the scientific pursuits being the highest intellectual calling—linked to the achievement of spiritual perfection. Andreae's island also depicts a great advancement in technology, with many industries separated in different zones which supplied the population's needs—which shows great resemblance to Bacon's scientific methods and purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-Farrington_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Farrington-94">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While rejecting occult <a href="/wiki/Conspiracy_theories" class="mw-redirect" title="Conspiracy theories">conspiracy theories</a> surrounding Bacon and the claim Bacon personally identified as a Rosicrucian, intellectual historian Paolo Rossi has argued for an occult influence on Bacon's scientific and religious writing. He argues that Bacon was familiar with early modern <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemical</a> texts and that Bacon's ideas about the application of science had roots in <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_magic" title="Renaissance magic">Renaissance magical</a> ideas about science and magic facilitating humanity's domination of nature.<sup id="cite_ref-Rossi_1968,_Chapter_1_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rossi_1968,_Chapter_1-96">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Rossi further interprets Bacon's search for hidden meanings in myth and fables in such texts as <i>The Wisdom of the Ancients</i> as succeeding earlier occultist and <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonic</a> attempts to locate hidden wisdom in pre-Christian myths.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> As indicated by the title of his study, however, Rossi claims Bacon ultimately rejected the philosophical foundations of occultism as he came to develop a form of modern science.<sup id="cite_ref-Rossi_1968,_Chapter_1_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rossi_1968,_Chapter_1-96">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Rossi's analysis and claims have been extended by Jason Josephson-Storm in his study, <i>The Myth of Disenchantment</i>. Josephson-Storm also rejects conspiracy theories surrounding Bacon and does not make the claim that Bacon was an active Rosicrucian. However, he argues that Bacon's "rejection" of magic actually constituted an attempt to purify magic of Catholic, demonic, and esoteric influences and to establish magic as a field of study and application paralleling Bacon's vision of science. Furthermore, Josephson-Storm argues that Bacon drew on magical ideas when developing his experimental method.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Josephson-Storm finds evidence that Bacon considered nature a living entity, populated by spirits, and argues Bacon's views on the human domination and application of nature actually depend on his spiritualism and personification of nature.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Rosicrucian organisation <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Mystical_Order_Rosae_Crucis" title="Ancient Mystical Order Rosae Crucis">AMORC</a> claims that Bacon was the "Imperator" (leader) of the Rosicrucian Order in both England and the European continent, and would have directed it during his lifetime.<sup id="cite_ref-HistoryofLife_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HistoryofLife-100">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacon's influence can also be seen on a variety of religious and spiritual authors, and on groups that have utilised his writings in their own belief systems.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bibliography">Bibliography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Bacon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Bibliography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon_bibliography" title="Francis Bacon bibliography">Francis Bacon bibliography</a></div>
<p>Some of the more notable works by Bacon are:
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Essays_(Francis_Bacon)" title="Essays (Francis Bacon)">Essays</a></i>
<ul><li>1st edition with 10 essays (1597)</li>
<li>2nd edition with 38 essays (1612)</li>
<li>3rd/final edition with 58 essays (1625)</li></ul></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Advancement_of_Learning" class="mw-redirect" title="Advancement of Learning">The Advancement and Proficience of Learning Divine and Human</a></i> (1605)</li>
<li><i>Instauratio magna</i> (The Great Instauration) (1620): a multi-part work including <i>Distributio operis</i> (plan of the work); <i>Novum Organum</i> (new engine); <i>Parasceve ad historiam naturalem</i>, (preparatory for natural history) and <i>Catalogus historiarum particularium </i>(catalogue of particular histories) <sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>De augmentis scientiarum (</i>1623) - an enlargement of <i>The Advancement of Learning</i> translated into Latin</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/New_Atlantis" title="New Atlantis">New Atlantis</a></i> (1626)</li></ul>

For man, by the fall, fell at the same time from his state of innocency and from his dominion over creation. Both of these losses however can even in this life be in some part repaired; the former by religion and faith, the latter by arts and sciences. – Francis Bacon, <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Novum_Organum" class="extiw" title="s:Novum Organum">Novum Organum</a></span>

Wherefore there shall cease all servitude, falsehood, lies, and darkness, which by little and little, with the great world's revolution, was crept into all arts, works, and governments of men, and have darkened most part of them".  – <a href="/wiki/Confessio_Fraternitatis" title="Confessio Fraternitatis">Confessio Fraternitatis</a></span>

<li id="cite_note-70"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-70">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Urbach, Peter (1987). <i>Francis Bacon's Philosophy of Science: An Account and a Reappraisal</i>.
